In the case of the Bail Reform Act, we placed weight on the fact that the statute applied only to defendants suspected of "the most serious of crimes," <em>Salerno</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 747; see also <em>Foucha</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 81, while the statute in <em>Kansas</em> v. <em>Hendricks,</em> 521 U.&nbsp;S. 346 (1997), likewise provided only for confinement of "a limited subclass of dangerous persons" who had committed "&nbsp;'a sexually violent offense'&nbsp;" and who suffered from "&nbsp;'a mental abnormality or personality disorder" portending "predatory acts of sexual violence,'&nbsp;" <em>id.</em>, at 357 (quoting Kan. Stat. Ann. 59-29a02(a) (1994)).  <em>Salerno</em> relied on the restriction of detention "by the stringent time limitations of the Speedy Trial Act," 406 U.&nbsp;S. 715, 738 (1972) ("At the least, due process requires that the nature and duration of commitment bear some reasonable relation to the purpose for which the individual is committed").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The substantive demands of due process necessarily go hand in hand with the procedural, and the cases insist at the least on an opportunity for a detainee to challenge the reason claimed for committing him.  <em>E. g.</em>, <em>Hendricks,</em> <em>supra</em>, at 357 (stating that civil commitment was permitted where "the confinement takes place pursuant to proper procedures and evidentiary standards"); <em>Foucha</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 81-82 (invalidating a statute under which "the State need prove nothing to justify continued detention"); <em>Salerno</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 751 ("[T]he procedures by which a judicial officer evaluates the likelihood of future dangerousness are specifically designed to further the accuracy of that determination"); <em>Addington</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 427 (requiring a heightened burden of proof "to impress the factfinder with the importance of the decision and thereby perhaps to reduce the chances that inappropriate commitments will be ordered").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;These cases yield a simple distillate that should govern the result here.  Due process calls for an individual determination before someone is locked away.  In none of the cases cited did we ever suggest that the government could avoid the Due Process Clause by doing what 1226(c) does, by selecting a class of people for confinement on a categorical basis and denying members of that class any chance to dispute the necessity of putting them away.  The cases, of course, would mean nothing if citizens and comparable residents could be shorn of due process by this sort of categorical sleight of hand.  Without any "full-blown adversary hearing" before detention, <em>Salerno</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 750, or heightened burden of proof, <em>Addington</em>, <em>supra</em>, or other procedures to show the government's interest in committing an individual, <em>Foucha</em>, <em>supra;</em> <em>Jackson</em>, <em>supra</em>, procedural rights would amount to nothing but mechanisms for testing group membership.  Cf. <em>Foucha</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 88 (opinion of <em>O'Connor</em>, J.) ("Nor would it be permissible to treat all acquittees alike, without regard for their particular crimes").  And if procedure could be dispensed with so expediently, so presumably could the substantive requirements that the class of detainees be narrow and the detention period strictly limited.  <em>Salerno</em>, <em>supra;</em> <em>Hendricks</em>, <em>supra</em>.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We held as much just two Terms ago in <em>Zadvydas</em> v. <em>Davis, </em>533 U.&nbsp;S. 678 (2001), which stands for the proposition that detaining an alien requires more than the rationality of a general detention statute; any justification must go to the alien himself.  <em>Zadvydas</em> considered detention of two aliens, Zadvydas and Ma, who had already been ordered removed and therefore enjoyed no lawful immigration status.  Their cases arose because actual removal appeared unlikely owing to the refusal of their native countries to accept them, with the result that they had been detained not only for the standard 90-day removal period, during which time most removal orders are executed, but beyond that period because the INS considered them to be a "&nbsp;'risk to the community'&nbsp;" and "&nbsp;'unlikely to comply with the order of removal.'&nbsp;"  <em>Id.</em>, at 682 (quoting 8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1231(a)(6) (1994 ed., Supp. V)).  Zadvydas and Ma challenged their continued and potentially indefinite detention under the Due Process Clause of the Fifth Amendment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The <em>Zadvydas</em> opinion opened by noting the clear applicability of general due process standards: physical detention requires both a "special justification" that "outweighs the 'individual's constitutionally protected interest in avoiding physical restraint'&nbsp;" and "adequate procedural protections."  533 U.&nbsp;S., at 690 (quoting <em>Hendricks</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 356).  Nowhere did we suggest that the "constitutionally protected liberty interest" in avoiding physical confinement, even for aliens already ordered removed, was conceptually different from the liberty interest of citizens considered in <em>Jackson</em>, <em>Salerno</em>, <em>Foucha</em>, and <em>Hendricks</em>.  On the contrary, we cited those cases and expressly adopted their reasoning, even as applied to aliens whose right to remain in the United States had already been declared forfeited.  <em>Zadvydas</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 690.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thus, we began by positing commonly accepted substantive standards and proceeded to enquire into any "special justification" that might outweigh the aliens' powerful interest in avoiding physical confinement "under [individually ordered] release conditions that may not be violated."  <em>Id.</em>, at 696.  We found nothing to justify the Government's position.  The statute was not narrowed to a particularly dangerous class of aliens, but rather affected "aliens ordered removed for many and various reasons, including tourist visa violations."  <em>Id.</em>, at 691.  The detention itself was not subject to "stringent time limitations," <em>Salerno</em>, 481 U.&nbsp;S., at 747, but was potentially indefinite or even permanent, <em>Zadvydas</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 691.  Finally, although both Zadvydas and Ma appeared to be dangerous, this conclusion was undermined by defects in the procedures resulting in the finding of dangerousness.  <em>Id.</em>, at 692.  The upshot was such serious doubt about the constitutionality of the detention statute that we construed it as authorizing continuing detention only when an alien's removal was "reasonably foreseeable."  <em>Id.</em>, at 699.  In the cases of Zadvydas and Ma, the fact that their countries of citizenship were not willing to accept their return weighed against the Government's interest in keeping them at hand for instant removal, even though both were serious flight risks, <em>id.</em>, at 684-686, 690, and we remanded the cases to the Courts of Appeals for a determination of the sufficiency of the Government's interests in Zadvydas's and Ma's individual detention, <em>id.</em>, at 702.  </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Our individualized analysis and disposition in <em>Zadvydas</em> support Kim's claim for an individualized review of his challenge to the reasons that are supposed to justify confining him prior to any determination of removability.  In fact, aliens in removal proceedings have an additional interest in avoiding confinement, beyond anything considered in <em>Zadvydas:</em> detention prior to entry of a removal order may well impede the alien's ability to develop and present his case on the very issue of removability.  See Brief for Citizens and Immigrants for Equal Justice et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 20-23.  After all, our recognition that the serious penalty of removal must be justified on a heightened standard of proof, <em>Woodby</em> v. <em>INS,</em> 385 U.&nbsp;S. 276 (1966), will not mean all that much when the INS can detain, transfer, and isolate aliens away from their lawyers, witnesses, and evidence.  Cf. <em>Stack</em> v. <em>Boyle,</em> 342 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 4 (1951).  Kim's right to defend against removal gives him an even stronger claim than the aliens in <em>Zadvydas</em> could raise.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In fact, the principal dissenters in <em>Zadvydas</em>, as well as the majority, accepted a theory that would compel success for Kim in this case.  The dissent relied on the fact that Zadvydas and Ma were subject to a "final order of removal" and had "no right under the basic immigration laws to remain in this country," 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 720 (opinion of <em>Kennedy</em>, J.), in distinguishing them "from aliens with a lawful right to remain here," <em>ibid.</em>, which is Kim's position.  The dissent recognized the right of all aliens, even "removable and inadmissible" ones, to be "free from detention that is arbitrary or capricious," <em>id.</em>, at 721, and the opinion explained that detention would pass the "arbitrary or capricious" test "when necessary to avoid the risk of flight or danger to the community," <em>ibid.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 10" name="FRother1.10" href="#FNother1.10">10</a></sup></em></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Hence the <em>Zadvydas </em>dissent's focus on "whether there are adequate procedures" allowing "persons once subject to detention to show that through rehabilitation, new appreciation of their responsibilities, or under other standards, they no longer present special risks or danger if put at large."  <em>Ibid.</em>; see also <em>id.</em>, at 722-723.  Indeed, there is further support for Kim's claim in the dissent's view that the process afforded to removable aliens like Zadvydas and Ma "[went] far toward th[e] objective" of satisfying procedural due process, <em>id.</em>, at 722;<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 11" name="FRother1.11" href="#FNother1.11">11</a></sup> that process stands in stark contrast to the total absence of custody review available in response to Kim's claim that he is neither dangerous nor a flight risk.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 12" name="FRother1.12" href="#FNother1.12">12</a></sup>  The removable aliens in <em>Zadvydas</em> had the right to a hearing, to representation, and to consideration of facts bearing on risk of flight, including criminal history, evidence of rehabilitation, and ties to the United States.  <em>Ibid.</em>  The references to the "necessity" of an individual's detention and the discussion of the procedural requirements show that the principal <em>Zadvydas</em> dissenters envisioned due process as individualized review, and the Court of Appeals in this case correctly held that Kim's mandatory detention without benefit of individualized enquiry violated due process as understood by both the <em>Zadvydas </em>majority and <em>Justice Kennedy</em> in dissent.  <em>Kim</em> v. <em>Ziglar</em>, 276 F.&nbsp;3d 523, 535-537 (CA9 2002).  Every Court of Appeals to consider the detention of an LPR under 1226(c) after <em>Zadvydas</em> reached the same conclusion.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 13" name="FRother1.13" href="#FNother1.13">13</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">D</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, due process requires a "special justification" for physical detention that "outweighs the individual's constitutionally protected interest in avoiding physical restraint&nbsp;" as well as "adequate procedural protections."  <em>Zadvydas</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 690-691 (internal quotation marks omitted).  "There must be a 'sufficiently compelling' governmental interest to justify such an action, usually a punitive interest in imprisoning the convicted criminal or a regulatory interest in forestalling danger to the community."  <em>Flores,</em> 507 U.&nbsp;S., at 316 (<em>O'Connor, </em>J., concurring) (quoting <em>Salerno</em>, 481 U.&nbsp;S., at 748).  The class of persons subject to confinement must be commensurately narrow and the duration of confinement limited accordingly.  <em>Zadvydas</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 691; <em>Hendricks</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S., at 368; <em>Foucha</em>, 504 U.&nbsp;S., at 81-82; <em>Salerno</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 747, 750.  <em>Justice Kennedy'</em>s dissenting view in <em>Zadvydas</em>, like that of the majority, disapproved detention that is not "necessary" to counter a risk of flight or danger; it is "arbitrary or capricious" and violates the substantive component of the Due Process Clause.  533 U.&nbsp;S., at 721.   Finally, procedural due process requires, at a minimum, that a detainee have the benefit of an impartial decisionmaker able to consider particular circumstances on the issue of necessity.  <em>Id.</em>, at 691-692; <em>id.</em>, at 722 (<em>Kennedy</em>, J., dissenting); <em>Foucha</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 81; <em>Salerno</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 750.  See also <em>Kenyeres</em> v. <em>Ashcroft</em>, <em>ante</em>, at ___ (slip op., at 4) (<em>Kennedy</em>, J., in chambers) ("An opportunity to present one's meritorious grievances to a court supports the legitimacy and public acceptance of a statutory regime").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;By these standards, Kim's case is an easy one.  "[H]eightened, substantive due process scrutiny," <em>Flores,</em> <em>supra</em>, at 316 (<em>O'Connor</em>, J., concurring), uncovers serious infirmities in 1226(c).  Detention is not limited to dangerous criminal aliens or those found likely to flee, but applies to all aliens claimed to be deportable for criminal convictions, even where the underlying offenses are minor.  <em>E. g.</em>, <em>Michel</em> v. <em>INS</em>, 206 F.&nbsp;3d 253, 256 (CA2 2000) (possession of stolen bus transfers); <em>Matter of Bart</em>, 20 I.&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;N. Dec. 436 (BIA 1992) (issuance of a bad check).  Detention under 1226(c) is not limited by the kind of time limit imposed by the Speedy Trial Act, and while it lasts only as long as the removal proceedings, those proceedings have no deadline and may last over a year.  See Brief for Citizens and Immigrants for Equal Justice et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 23-26; see also <em>id.</em>, at 10-20 (citing examples).  Section 1226(c) neither requires nor permits an official to determine whether Kim's detention was necessary to prevent flight or danger.  </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Kim's detention without particular justification in these respects, or the opportunity to enquire into it, violates both components of due process, and I would accordingly affirm the judgment of the Court of Appeals requiring the INS to hold a bail hearing to see whether detention is needed to avoid a risk of flight or a danger to the community.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 14" name="FRother1.14" href="#FNother1.14">14</a></sup>  This is surely little enough, given the fact that 8  U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1536 gives an LPR charged with being a foreign terrorist the right to a release hearing pending a determination that he be removed.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court proceeds to the contrary conclusion on the premise that "the Government may constitutionally detain deportable aliens during the limited period necessary for their removal proceedings."  <em>Ante</em>, at 16.  Sometimes, maybe often, it may, but that is not the point in contention.  Kim has never challenged the INS's general power to detain aliens in removal proceedings or even its power to detain him in particular, if it affords him a chance to participate in an enquiry whether he poses a flight risk or a danger to society.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The question, rather, is whether Congress has chosen "&nbsp;'a constitutionally permissible means of implementing' [its immigration] power."  <em>Zadvydas</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 695 (quoting <em>INS</em> v. <em>Chadha,</em> 462 U.&nbsp;S. 919, 941-942 (1983)); see also <em>Carlson</em> v. <em>Landon,</em> 342 U.&nbsp;S. 524, 537 (1952) (stating that the deportation power "is, of course, subject to judicial intervention under the 'paramount law of the Constitution'&nbsp;").  As in <em>Zadvydas</em>, we are here concerned not with the power to remove aliens but with the "important constitutional limitations" on that power's exercise.  <em>Zadvydas</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 695.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 15" name="FRother1.15" href="#FNother1.15">15</a></sup>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court spends much effort trying to distinguish <em>Zadvydas</em>, but even if the Court succeeded, success would not avail it much.  <em>Zadvydas</em> was an application of principles developed in over a century of cases on the rights of aliens and the limits on the government's power to confine individuals.  While there are differences between detention pending removal proceedings (this case) and detention after entry of a removal order <em>(Zadvydas)</em>, the differences merely point up that Kim's is the stronger claim, see <em>supra</em>, at 15-17.  In any case, the analytical framework set forth in <em>Salerno</em>, <em>Foucha</em>, <em>Hendricks</em>, <em>Jackson</em>, and other physical confinement cases applies to both, and the two differences the Court relies upon fail to remove Kim's challenge from the ambit of either the earlier cases or <em>Zadvydas</em> itself.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 16" name="FRother1.16" href="#FNother1.16">16</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;First, the Court says that 1226(c) "serves the purpose of preventing deportable criminal aliens from fleeing prior to or during their removal proceedings."  <em>Ante</em>, at 17.  Yes it does, and the statute in <em>Zadvydas</em>, viewed outside the context of any individual alien's detention, served the purpose of preventing aliens ordered to be deported from fleeing prior to actual deportation.  In each case, the fact that a statute serves its purpose in general fails to justify the detention of an individual in particular.  Some individual aliens covered by 1226(c) have meritorious challenges to removability or claims for relief from removal.  See Brief for Citizens and Immigrants for Equal Justice et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 10-20.  As to such aliens, as with Zadvydas and Ma, the Government has only a weak reason under the immigration laws for detaining them.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court appears to respond that Congress may require detention of removable aliens based on a general conclusion that detention is needed for effective removal of criminal aliens on a class-wide basis.  But on that logic <em>Zadvydas</em> should have come out the other way, for detention of the entire class of aliens who have actually been ordered removed will in general "serv[e] the purpose" of their effective removal, <em>ante</em>, at 17.  Yet neither the Court nor <em>Justice Kennedy</em> in dissent suggested that scrutiny under the Due Process Clause could be satisfied at such a general level.  Rather, we remanded the individual cases of Zadvydas and Ma for determinations of the strength of the Government's reasons for detaining them in particular.  533 U.&nbsp;S., at 702.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 17" name="FRother1.17" href="#FNother1.17">17</a></sup>  We can insist on nothing less here, since the Government's justification for detaining individuals like Zadvydas and Ma, who had no right to remain in this country and were proven flight risks and dangers to society, <em>id.</em>, at 684-686, is certainly stronger (and at least no weaker) than its interest in detaining a lawful permanent resident who has not been shown (or even claimed) to be either a flight risk or a threat to the community.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 18" name="FRother1.18" href="#FNother1.18">18</a></sup>  </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's closest approach to a reason justifying class-wide detention without exception here is a Senate Report stating that over 20% of nondetained criminal aliens failed to appear for removal hearings.  <em>Ante</em>, at 8 (citing S.&nbsp;Rep. No. 104-48 (1995) (hereinafter Senate Report)).  To begin with, the Senate Report's statistic treats all criminal aliens alike and does not distinguish between LPRs like Kim, who are likely to have developed strong ties within the United States, see <em>supra</em>, at 5-9, and temporary visitors or illegal entrants.  Even more importantly, the statistic tells us nothing about flight risk at all because, as both the Court and the Senate Report recognize, the INS was making its custody determinations not on the ground of likelihood of flight or dangerousness, but "in large part, according to the number of beds available in a particular region."  Senate Report, at&nbsp;23, cited <em>ante</em>, at 8; see also H.&nbsp;R. Rep. No. 104-469, p. 124 (1995) (hereinafter House Report) ("[I]n deciding to release a deportable alien, the INS is making a decision that the alien cannot be detained given its limited resources"); App. 26-27.  This meant that the INS often could not detain even the aliens who posed serious flight risks.  Senate Report, at&nbsp;23 (noting that the INS had only 3,500 detention beds for criminal aliens in the entire country and the INS district comprising Pennsylvania, Delaware, and West Virginia had only 15).  The desperate lack of detention space likewise had led the INS to set bonds too low, because "if the alien is not able to pay, the alien cannot be released, and a needed bed space is lost."  House Report, at 124.  The Senate Report also recognized that, even when the INS identifies a criminal alien, the INS "often refuses to take action because of insufficient agents to transport prisoners, or because of limited detention space."  Senate Report, at 2.  Four former high-ranking INS officials explained the Court's statistics as follows: "Flight rates were so high in the early 1990s not as a result of chronic discretionary judgment failures by [the] INS in assessing which aliens might pose a flight risk.  Rather, the rates were alarmingly high because decisions to release aliens in proceedings were driven overwhelmingly by a lack of detention facilities."  Brief for T. Alexander Aleinikoff et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 19. </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's recognition that, at the time of the enactment of 1226(c), "individualized bail determinations had not been tested under optimal conditions" is thus rather an understatement.  <em>Ante</em>, at 17.  The Court does not explain how the INS's resource-driven decisions to release individuals who pose serious flight risks, and their predictable failure to attend removal hearings, could justify a systemwide denial of any opportunity for release to individuals like Kim who are neither flight risks nor threats to the public.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court also cites a report by the Department of Justice relied upon by the Government.  Department of Justice, Office of the Inspector General, Immigration and Naturalization Service, Deportation of Aliens After Final Orders Have Been Issued, Rep. No. I-96-03 (Mar. 1996)<br>(hereinafter Post-Order Report), App. 14, cited <em>ante</em>, at 7-<br>8, 10.  But that report does not even address the issue of detention before a determination has been made that an alien is removable.  As its title indicates, the Post-Order Report analyzed removal rates only for aliens who had already received final orders of removability.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 19" name="FRother1.19" href="#FNother1.19">19</a></sup>  See also <em>id.</em>, at 25 ("This current review was limited to actions taken by INS to remove aliens after [immigration judges or the Board of Immigration Appeals] had issued final orders").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 20" name="FRother1.20" href="#FNother1.20">20</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;More relevant to this case, and largely ignored by the Court, is a recent study conducted at the INS's request concluding that 92% of criminal aliens (most of whom were LPRs) who were released under supervisory conditions attended all of their hearings.  1 Vera Institute of Justice, Testing Community Supervision for the INS: An Evaluation of the Appearance Assistance Program, pp. ii, 33, 36 (Aug. 1, 2000) (hereinafter Vera Institute Study).  Even without supervision, 82% of criminal aliens released on recognizance showed up, as did 77% of those released on bond, leading the reporters to conclude that "supervision was especially effective for criminal aliens" and that "mandatory detention of virtually all criminal aliens is not necessary."  <em>Id.</em>, at ii, 36, 42.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 21" name="FRother1.21" href="#FNother1.21">21</a></sup>  </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court nowhere addresses the Vera Institute's conclusion that criminal aliens released under supervisory conditions are overwhelmingly likely to attend their hearings.  Instead, the Court fixes on the fact that 23% of the comparison group of aliens released on bond failed to attend all of their hearings.  <em>Ante</em>, at 8-9.  Since the bond determinations were made by the INS, the fact remains that resource-driven concerns may well have led the INS to release individuals who were evident flight risks on bonds too low to ensure their attendance.  See <em>supra</em>, at 24-25.  The Court's assumption that the INS's bond determinations involved "individualized screening" for flight risk, <em>ante</em>, at 9, finds no support in the Vera Institute Study.  Thus the Court's reliance on the failure rate of aliens released by the INS on bond, whether it comes from the Senate Report or the Vera Institute Study, <em>ante</em>, at 8-<br>9, does not support its conclusion.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, the Court's inapposite statistics do not show that detention of criminal LPRs pending removal proceedings, even on a general level, is necessary to ensure attendance at removal hearings, and the Vera Institute Study reinforces the point by establishing the effectiveness of release under supervisory conditions, just as we did in <em>Zadvydas</em>.  533 U.&nbsp;S., at 696 (noting that imprisonment was constitutionally suspect given the possibility of "supervision under release conditions that may not be violated").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 22" name="FRother1.22" href="#FNother1.22">22</a></sup>  The Court's first attempt to distinguish <em>Zadvydas</em> accordingly fails.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's second effort is its claim that mandatory detention under 1226(c) is generally of a "much shorter duration" than the incarceration at issue in <em>Zadvydas</em>.  <em>Ante</em>, at 18.  While it is true that removal proceedings are unlikely to prove "indefinite and potentially permanent," 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 696, they are not formally limited to any period, and often extend beyond the time suggested by the Court, that is, "&nbsp;an average time of 47 days" or, for aliens who exercise their right of appeal, "an average of four months."  <em>Ante</em>, at 19; see also Case Hearing Report 12 (finding that the average time from receipt of charging documents by a detained alien to a final decision by the immigration judge was 54 days).  Even taking these averages on their face, however, they are no legitimate answer to the due process claim to individualized treatment and hearing.  </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the first place, the average time from receipt of charging documents to decision obscures the fact that the alien may receive charging documents only after being detained for a substantial period.  Kim, for example, was not charged until five weeks after the INS detained him.  Brief for Respondent 9.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even more revealing is an explanation of the raw numbers that are averaged out.  As the Solicitor General conceded, the length of the average detention period in great part reflects the fact that the vast majority of cases involve aliens who raise no challenge to removability at all.  Tr. of Oral Arg. 57.  LPRs like Kim, however, will hardly fit that pattern.  Unlike many illegal entrants and temporary nonimmigrants, LPRs are the aliens most likely to press substantial challenges to removability requiring lengthy proceedings.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 23" name="FRother1.23" href="#FNother1.23">23</a></sup>  See Vera Institute Study 33, 37 (stating that many of the criminal aliens studied were "lawful permanent residents who have spent much or all of their adult lives in the United States" and that 40% of those released on supervision "were allowed to remain in the United States").  Successful challenges often require several months of proceedings, see Brief for Citizens and Immigrants for Equal Justice et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 10-<br>20; detention for an open-ended period like this falls far short of the "stringent time limitations" held to be significant in <em>Salerno</em>, 481 U.&nbsp;S., at 747.  The potential for several months of confinement requires an individualized finding of necessity under <em>Zadvydas</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 24" name="FRother1.24" href="#FNother1.24">24</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court has failed to distinguish <em>Zadvydas </em>in any way that matters.  It does no better in its effort to portray its result in this case as controlled by <em>Carlson</em> v. <em>Landon,</em> 342 U.&nbsp;S. 524 (1952), and <em>Reno</em> v. <em>Flores,</em> 507 U.&nbsp;S. 292 (1993).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>
<em>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Carlson</em> did not involve mandatory detention.  It involved a system similar to the one Kim contends for here.  The aliens' detention pending deportation proceedings in <em>Carlson </em>followed a decision on behalf of the Attorney General that custody was preferable to release on bond or on conditional parole.  <em>Carlson</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 528, n.&nbsp;5 (citing Internal Security Act of 1950, 23, 64 Stat. 1011).  We sustained that decision because we found that the District Director of the INS, to whom the Attorney General had delegated the authority, did not abuse his discretion in concluding that "evidence of membership [in the Communist Party] plus personal activity in supporting and extending the Party's philosophy concerning violence" made the aliens "a menace to the public interest."  342 U.&nbsp;S., at 541.  The significance of looking to "personal activity" in our analysis was complemented by our express recognition that there was "no evidence or contention that all persons arrested as deportable ... for Communist membership are denied bail," <em>id.</em>, at 541-542, and by a Government report showing that in fact "the large majority" of aliens arrested on charges comparable to the <em>Carlson</em> petitioners' were allowed bail.  <em>Id.</em>, at 542; see also <em>id.</em>, at 538, n.&nbsp;31 (noting that it was "quite clear" that "detention without bond has been the exception").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Indeed, the <em>Carlson </em>Court's constitutional analysis relying on the opportunity for individualized bond determinations simply followed the argument in the brief for the United States in that case.  In response to the aliens' argument that the statute made it "mandatory on the Attorney General to deny bail to alien communists," the Government stated, "[w]e need not consider the constitutionality of such a law for that is not what the present law provides."  Brief for Respondent in <em>Carlson</em> v. <em>Landon</em>, O.&nbsp;T. 1951, No. 35, p.&nbsp;19; see also <em>id.</em>, at 20 ("[T]he act itself, by its terms, leaves no doubt that the power to detain is discretionary, not mandatory").  The Government also presented the following excerpt of a statement of the chairman of the House Judiciary Committee:</p>
<blockquote><p>"&nbsp;'No particular hardship is going to be worked on anyone because, bear this fact in mind, <em>it is not mandatory on the Attorney General to hold people in detention.  He is given discretionary power.</em>  If in his judgment one of the class of people I have just mentioned ought to be held for paramount national reasons, he may detain him, but he is not obliged to hold anybody, <em>although I trust that in every case of a subversive or a hardened criminal he will.</em>'&nbsp;" <em>Id.</em>, at 19 (quoting 96 Cong. Rec. 10449-10450 (1950) (statement of Rep. Walter) (emphasis added in Brief for Respondent in <em>Carlson</em> v. <em>Landon</em>, <em>supra</em>)).</p>
</blockquote><p>
In short, <em>Carlson</em> addressed a very different scheme from the one here.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is also beside the point for the Court to suggest that "like respondent in the present case," the <em>Carlson</em> petitioners challenged their detention because "there had been no finding that they were unlikely to appear for their deportation proceedings."  <em>Ante</em>, at 13.  Each of them was detained after being found to be "a menace to the public interest," 342 U.&nbsp;S., at 541, and their challenge, unlike Kim's, was that the INS had locked them up for an impermissible reason (danger to society) whereas only a finding of risk of flight would have justified detention.  <em>Id.</em>, at 533-534 ("It is urged ... that where there is no evidence to justify a fear of unavailability for the hearings or for the carrying out of a possible judgment of deportation, denial of bail under the circumstances of these cases is an abuse of discretion"); see also <em>id.</em>, at 551 (Black, J., dissenting) ("A power to put in jail because dangerous cannot be derived from a power to deport").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 25" name="FRother1.25" href="#FNother1.25">25</a></sup>  We rejected that contention, leaving the petitioners in detention because they were dangerous to the public interest, and on that issue, an official had determined that the <em>Carlson </em>petitioners ought to be detained.  Here, however, no impartial decisionmaker has determined that detaining Kim is required for any purpose at all, and neither the Government nor the Court even claims such a need.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For the same reason it is beside the point to note that the unsuccessful <em>Carlson</em> petitioners' brief raised a claim that detention without reference to facts personal to their individual cases would violate the Due Process Clause.  <em>Ante</em>, at 13.  As the United States pointed out in its own <em>Carlson</em> brief, that issue was never presented, since the District Director's exercise of discretion was based on individualized determinations that the petitioners were dangerous to society.  See <em>supra</em>, at 31-32.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 26" name="FRother1.26" href="#FNother1.26">26</a></sup>  Nor is the Court entitled to invoke <em>Carlson</em> by saying that the INS "had adopted a policy of refusing to grant bail" to alien Communists, which made the Attorney General's discretion to release aliens on bond merely "ostensibl[e]."  <em>Ante</em>, at 13.  The <em>Carlson</em> Court found that "[t]here is no evidence or contention that all persons arrested as deportable ... for Communist membership are denied bail."  342 U.&nbsp;S., at 541-542.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court refuses to accept the opinion of the <em>Carlson</em> Court and the representations made in the successful brief for the Government in that case.  The Court not only fails to acknowledge the actual holding of <em>Carlson</em>; it improperly adopts as authority statements made in dissent.  The Court's emphatic assertion that "[t]here was no 'individualized findin[g]' of likely future dangerousness as to any of the aliens," <em>ante</em>, at 14, rests entirely on opinions voiced in dissent, although the Court only mentions this fact in a footnote, <em>ante</em>, at 14, n.&nbsp;8 (citing 342 U.&nbsp;S., at 549, 551, n.&nbsp;5, 552 (Black, J., dissenting), and <em>id.</em>, at 567 (Frankfurter, J., dissenting)).  Statements made in dissent do not override the <em>Carlson </em>Court's express finding that the petitioners in that case were found to be not only members of the Communist Party, but "active in Communist work" and to "a degree, minor perhaps in [one] case, [participants] in Communist activities."  <em>Id.</em>, at 541.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 27" name="FRother1.27" href="#FNother1.27">27</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Moreover, the <em>Carlson </em>dissenters did not suggest that no individualized determinations had occurred; rather, they contended that the District Director's individual findings of dangerousness were unsupported by sufficient reliable evidence.  See <em>id.</em>, at 549-550 (Black, J., dissenting) (arguing that the aliens were not in fact "&nbsp;'dangerous'&nbsp;" at all); <em>id.</em>, at 552 (arguing that danger findings were based on "the rankest hearsay evidence" instead of the INS being "required to prove" that the detainee was dangerous);  <em>id.</em>, at 555-556 (arguing that activity within the Communist movement did not make the aliens "dangerous"); <em>id.</em>, at 566-567 (Frankfurter, J., dissenting) (arguing that evidence of Communist party membership was "insufficient to show danger"; that evidence of some aliens' activities was stale; and that the history of treatment of the aliens involved forced him to conclude that the Attorney General was not actually exercising discretion on an individual basis).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 28" name="FRother1.28" href="#FNother1.28">28</a></sup>  And even if the <em>Carlson</em> dissenters were factually correct, all that would show is that the <em>Carlson</em> Court was misled (by the Government, no less) into deciding the case on the basis that individualized findings of dangerousness were made.  Given that the <em>Carlson</em> Court clearly believed that it was deciding a case in which individualized determinations occurred, it is serious error for this Court to treat <em>Carlson</em> as deciding a case in which they did not.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, the Court gets no help from the isolated passages of the <em>Carlson </em>opinion that it quotes.  Although the <em>Carlson </em>Court stated that detention was "&nbsp;'a part'&nbsp;" of deportation procedure, <em>ante</em>, at 13 (quoting <em>Carlson</em>, 502 U.&nbsp;S. 183, 194-195 (1991); see also <em>Flores,</em> 507 U.&nbsp;S., at 313.  <em>Carlson</em> stands at odds with the Court's outcome in this case. </p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's paragraph on <em>Flores,</em> <em>supra</em>, is no more help to it.  Like <em>Carlson</em>, <em>Flores</em> did not involve mandatory detention, and the INS regulation at issue in <em>Flores</em> actually required that alien juveniles be released pending removal proceedings unless the INS determined that detention was required "&nbsp;'to secure [the juvenile's] timely appearance before the [INS] or the immigration court or to ensure the juvenile's safety or that of others.'&nbsp;"  507 U.&nbsp;S., at 297 (quoting 8 CFR 242.24(b)(1) (1992)).  Again, Kim agrees that such a system is constitutional and contends for it here.  <em>Flores</em> turned not on the necessity of detention, but on the regulation's restriction that alien juveniles could only be released to the custody of the juvenile's parent, legal guardian, or another specified adult relative.  Even this limitation, however, was subject to exception for releasing a juvenile to another person in "&nbsp;'unusual and compelling circumstances and in the discretion of the [INS] district director or chief patrol agent.'&nbsp;" 507 U.&nbsp;S., at 297 (quoting 8 CFR 242.24(b)(4) (1992)).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thus, the substantive due process issue in <em>Flores</em> was not whether the aliens' detention was necessary to a governmental purpose: "&nbsp;'freedom from physical restraint'&nbsp;" was "not at issue" at all because, as juveniles, the aliens were "&nbsp;'always in some form of custody.'&nbsp;"  467 U.&nbsp;S. 253, 265 (1984)).  Since "&nbsp;'[l]egal custody' rather than 'detention' more accurately describes the reality of the arrangement" in <em>Flores</em>, 507 U.&nbsp;S., at 298, that case has no bearing on this one, which concerns the detention of an adult.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 29" name="FRother1.29" href="#FNother1.29">29</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Flores</em> is equally distinguishable at the procedural level.  We held that the procedures for the custody decision sufficed constitutionally because any determination to keep the alien "in the custody of the [INS], released on recognizance, or released under bond" was open to review by the immigration court, the Board of Immigration Appeals, and the federal courts.  <em>Id.</em>, at 308.  Like the aliens in <em>Carlson</em>, the juveniles in <em>Flores</em> were subject to a different system and raised a different complaint from Kim's.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;While <em>Flores </em>holds that the INS may use "reasonable presumptions and generic rules" in carrying out its statutory discretion, <em>id.</em>, at 313, it gave no <em>carte blanche</em> to general legislation depriving an entire class of aliens of liberty during removal proceedings.  <em>Flores</em> did not disturb established standards that detention of an adult must be justified in each individual instance.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 30" name="FRother1.30" href="#FNother1.30">30</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">IV</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This case is not about the National Government's undisputed power to detain aliens in order to avoid flight or prevent danger to the community.  The issue is whether that power may be exercised by detaining a still lawful permanent resident alien when there is no reason for it and no way to challenge it.  The Court's holding that the Due Process Clause allows this under a blanket rule is devoid of even ostensible justification in fact and at odds with the settled standard of liberty.  I respectfully dissent.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<a name="other1"></a>



<center><h2><p align="CENTER">CHARLES DEMORE, DISTRICT DIRECTOR, SAN FRANCISCO DISTRICT OF IMMIGRATION AND NATURALIZATION SERVICE, <em>et&nbsp;al.</em>, PETI-<br>TIONERS <em>v.</em> HYUNG JOON KIM</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER">on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the ninth circuit</p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[April 29, 2003]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Justice Breyer</em>, concurring in part and dissenting in part.</p>
<p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I agree with the majority that the courts have jurisdiction, and I join Part I of its opinion.  If I believed (as the majority apparently believes, see <em>ante</em>, at 2-3, and n. 3) that Kim had conceded that he is deportable, then I would conclude that the Government could detain him without bail for the few weeks ordinarily necessary for formal entry of a removal order.  Brief for Petitioners 39-40; see <em>ante,</em> at 18-20.  Time limits of the kind set forth in <em>Zadvydas </em>v.<em> Davis</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S. 678 (2001), should govern these and longer periods of detention, for an alien's concession that he is deportable seems to me the rough equivalent of the entry of an order of removal.  See <em>id.</em>, at 699-701 (reading the statute, under constitutional compulsion, as commonly imposing a presumption of a six month "reasonable" time limit for post-removal-order detention).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This case, however, is not one in which an alien concedes deportability.  As <em>Justice Souter </em>points out, Kim argues to the contrary.  See <em>ante</em>, at 2-4 (opinion concurring in part and dissenting in part).  Kim claims that his earlier convictions were neither for an "&nbsp;'aggravated felony,'&nbsp;" nor for two crimes of "&nbsp;'moral turpitude.'&nbsp;"  Brief for Respondent 3, 11-12, 31-32, and n. 29.  And given shifting lower court views on such matters, I cannot say that his arguments are insubstantial or interposed solely for purposes of delay.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v.<em> Corona-Sanchez</em>, 291 F. 3d 1201, 1213 (CA9 2002) (petty theft with a prior not an "aggravated felony").  Compare <em>Omagah </em>v.<em> Ashcroft, </em>288 F. 3d 254, 259<em> (</em>CA5 2002) ("&nbsp;'Moral turpitude refers generally to conduct that shocks the public conscience as being inherently base, vile, or depraved'&nbsp;"), with <em>Guarneri</em> v.<em> Kessler,</em> 98 F. 2d 580, 580-581 (CA5 1938) ("Moral turpitude" involves "&nbsp;'[a]nything done contrary to justice, honesty, principle or good morals'&nbsp;"), and <em>Quilodran-Brau </em>v.<em> Holland, </em>232 F. 2d 183, 184 (CA3 1956) ("The borderline of 'moral turpitude' is not an easy one to locate").</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That being so -- as long as Kim's legal arguments are neither insubstantial nor interposed solely for purposes of delay -- then the immigration statutes, interpreted in light of the Constitution, permit Kim (if neither dangerous nor a flight risk) to obtain bail.  For one thing, Kim's constitutional claims to bail in these circumstances are strong.  See <em>ante,</em> at 10-14, 19-20 (<em>Souter</em>, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part).  Indeed, they are strong enough to require us to "ascertain whether a construction of the statute is fairly possible by which the [constitutional] question may be avoided."  <em>Crowell</em> v.<em> Benson</em>, 285 U. S. 22, 62 (1932); accord, <em>Zadvydas</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 689.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For another, the relevant statutes literally say nothing about an individual who, armed with a strong argument against deportability, might, or might not, fall within their terms.  Title 8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1226(c) tells the Attorney General to "take into custody any alien who . . . <em>is</em> deportable" (emphasis added), not one who may, or may not, fall into that category.  Indeed, the Government now permits such an alien to obtain bail if his argument against deportability is significantly <em>stronger</em> than substantial, <em>i.e.</em>, strong enough to make it "substantially unlikely" that the Government will win.  <em>In re Joseph</em>, 22 I. &amp; N. Dec. 799 (1999).  Cf. 8 CFR 3.19(h)(2)(ii) (2002).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, bail standards drawn from the criminal justice system are available to fill this statutory gap.  Federal law makes bail available to a criminal defendant after conviction and pending appeal provided (1) the appeal is "not for the purpose of delay," (2) the appeal "raises a substantial question of law or fact," and (3) the defendant shows by "clear and convincing evidence" that, if released, he "is not likely to flee or pose a danger to the safety" of the community.  18 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 3143(b).  These standards give considerable weight to any special governmental interest in detention (<em>e.g.</em>, process-related concerns or class-related flight risks, see <em>ante</em>, at 17).  The standards are more protective of a detained alien's liberty interest than those currently administered in the INS' <em>Joseph </em>hearings.  And they have proved workable in practice in the criminal justice system.  Nothing in the statute forbids their use when 1226(c) deportability is in doubt.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I would interpret the (silent) statute as imposing these bail standards. Cf. <em>Zadvydas</em>, <em>supra,</em> at 698; <em>United States </em>v.<em> Witkovich</em>, 353 U. S. 194, 201-202 (1957); <em>Kent </em>v.<em> Dulles</em>, 357 U.&nbsp;S. 116, 129 (1958).  So interpreted, the statute would require the Government to permit a detained alien to seek an individualized assessment of flight risk and dangerousness as long as the alien's claim that he is not deportable is (1) not interposed solely for purposes of delay and (2) raises a question of "law or fact" that is not insubstantial.  And that interpretation, in my view, is consistent with what the Constitution demands.  I would remand this case to the Ninth Circuit to determine whether Kim has raised such a claim. </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;With respect, I dissent from the Court's contrary <br>disposition.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;App. to Pet. for Cert. 32a; see 8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1101(a)(43)(G), 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii).  Section 1226(c) authorizes detention of aliens who have committed certain crimes including, <em>inter alia</em>, any "aggravated felony," 1226(c)(1)(B), 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), and any two "crimes involving moral turpitude," 1226(c)(1)(B), 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii).  Although the INS initially included only respondent's 1997 conviction in the charging document, it subsequently amended the immigration charges against him to include his 1996 conviction for first-degree burglary as another basis for mandatory detention and deportation.  Brief for Petitioners 3, n.&nbsp;2 (alleging that respondent's convictions reflected two "&nbsp;'crimes involving moral turpitude'&nbsp;").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As respondent explained: "The statute requires the [INS] to take into custody any alien who 'is deportable' from the United States based on having been convicted of any of a wide range of crimes...&nbsp;.  [Respondent] does not challenge INS's authority to take him into custody after he finished serving his criminal sentence.  His challenge is solely to Section 1226(c)'s absolute prohibition on his release from detention, even where, as here, the INS never asserted that he posed a danger or significant flight risk."  Brief in Opposition 1-2.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This "<em>Joseph</em> hearing" is immediately provided to a detainee who claims that he is not covered by 1226(c).  Tr. of Oral Arg. 22.  At the hearing, the detainee may avoid mandatory detention by demonstrating that he is not an alien, was not convicted of the predicate crime, or that the INS is otherwise substantially unlikely to establish that he is in fact subject to mandatory detention.  See 8 CFR 3.19(h)(2)(ii) (2002); <em>In re Joseph</em>, 22 I. &amp; N. Dec. 799 (1999).  Because respondent conceded that he was deportable because of a conviction that triggers 1226(c) and thus sought no <em>Joseph</em> hearing, we have no occasion to review the adequacy of <em>Joseph</em> hearings generally in screening out those who are improperly detained pursuant to 1226(c).  Such individualized review is available, however, and the dissent is mistaken if it means to suggest otherwise.  See <em>post</em>, at 17, 20 (opinion of <em>Souter</em>, J.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although the Attorney General had authority to release these aliens on bond, it is not clear that <em>all</em> of the aliens released were in fact given individualized bond hearings.  See Brief for Petitioners 19 ("[M]ore than 20% of criminal aliens who were released on bond <em>or otherwise not kept in custody</em> throughout their deportation proceedings failed to appear for those proceedings"), citing S. Rep. 104-48, at 2 (emphasis added).  The evidence does suggest, however, that <em>many</em> deportable criminal aliens in this "released criminal aliens" sample received such determinations.  See Brief for Petitioners 19 (noting that, for aliens not evaluated for flight risk at a bond hearing, the prehearing skip rate doubled to 40%).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent also claims that the study demonstrated that "92% of criminal aliens ... who were released under supervisory conditions attended all of their hearings."  <em>Post</em>, at 27 (opinion of <em>Souter</em>, J.).  The study did manage to raise the appearance rate for criminal aliens through a supervision program known as the Appearance Assistance Program (AAP).  But the AAP study is of limited value.  First, the study included only 16 aliens who, like respondent, were released from prison and charged with being deportable on the basis of an aggravated felony.  1 Vera Institute of Justice, Testing Community Supervision for the INS: An Evaluation of the Appearance Assistance Program, pp.&nbsp;33-34, 36 (Aug. 1, 2000).  In addition, all 127 aliens in the AAP study were admitted into the study group only after being screened for "strength of family and community ties, appearance rates in prior legal proceedings, and eligibility to apply for a legal remedy."  <em>Id</em>., at 13; see also <em>id</em>., at 37.  Following this selection process, "supervision staff were in frequent, ongoing communication with participants," <em>id</em>., at 14, through, among other things, required reporting sessions, periodic home visits, and assistance in retaining legal representation.  <em>Id., </em>at 41-42.  And, in any event, respondent seeks an individualized bond hearing, not "community supervision." The dissent's claim that criminal aliens released under supervisory conditions are likely to attend their hearings, <em>post</em>, at 27, therefore, is totally beside the point.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Respondent's concession on this score is relevant for two reasons:  First, because of the concession, respondent by his own choice did not receive one of the procedural protections otherwise provided to aliens detained under 1226(c).  And, second, because of the concession we do not reach a contrary argument raised by respondent for the first time in his brief on the merits in this Court.  Specifically, in his brief on the merits, respondent suggests that he might not be subject to detention under 1226(c) after all because his 1997 conviction for petty theft with priors might not qualify as an aggravated felony under recent Ninth Circuit precedent.  Respondent now states that he intends to argue at his next removal hearing that "his 1997 conviction does not constitute an aggravated felony ... and his 1996 conviction [for first-degree burglary] does not constitute either an aggravated felony or a crime involving moral turpitude."  Brief for Respondent 11-12.  As respondent has conceded that he is deportable for purposes of his habeas corpus challenge to 1226(c) at all previous stages of this proceeding, see n.&nbsp;3, <em>supra, </em>we decide the case on that basis.  Lest there be any confusion, we emphasize that by conceding he is "<em>deportable</em>" and, hence, subject to mandatory detention under 1226(c), respondent did not concede that he <em>will ultimately be deported</em>.  As the dissent notes, respondent has applied for withholding of removal. <em>Post,</em> at&nbsp;3 (opinion of <em>Souter</em>, J.). </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In fact, prior to 1907 there was no provision permitting bail for <em>any</em> aliens during the pendency of their deportation proceedings.  See 34 Stat. 905, 20.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Carlson</em> v. <em>Landon,</em> 342 U.&nbsp;S. 524, 549 (1952) (Black, J., dissenting) (noting that, in at least one case, the alien involved had been found "&nbsp;'<em>not</em> likely to engage in any subversive activities'&nbsp;" (emphasis added)); see also <em>id.,</em> at 550, n.&nbsp;5 (quoting the District Judge's finding in case No. 35 that "&nbsp;'I don't know whether it is true ... that their release is dangerous to the security of the United States'&nbsp;"); <em>id.,</em> at 552 ("[T]he bureau agent is <em>not</em> required to prove that a person he throws in jail is ... 'dangerous'&nbsp;" (emphasis added)); see also <em>id.,</em> at 567 (Frankfurter, J., dissenting) ("[T]he Attorney General ... did <em>not</em> deny bail from an individualized estimate of 'the danger to the public safety of [each person's] presence within the community'&nbsp;" (emphasis added)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Apart from its error with respect to the dangerousness determination, the dissent attempts to distinguish <em>Carlson</em> from the present case by arguing that the aliens in <em>Carlson</em> had engaged in "&nbsp;'personal activity'&nbsp;" in support of a political party Congress considered "&nbsp;'a menace to the public.'&nbsp;"  <em>Post</em>, at 31 (opinion of <em>Souter</em>, J.).  In suggesting that this is a distinction, the dissent ignores the "personal activity" that aliens like respondent have undertaken in committing the crimes that subject them to detention in the first instance--personal activity that has been determined with far greater procedural protections than any finding of "active membership" in the Communist Party involved in <em>Carlson</em>.  See 533 U.&nbsp;S. 678, 714 (2001) (<em>Kennedy</em>, J., dissenting) (noting that "a criminal record accumulated by an admitted alien" is a good indicator of future danger, and that "[a]ny suggestion that aliens who have completed prison terms no longer present a danger simply does not accord with the reality that a significant risk may still exist").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent denies this point, insisting that the detention at issue in <em>Zadvydas</em> actually did bear a reasonable relation to its immigration purpose.  <em>Post,</em> at 23 (opinion of <em>Souter</em>, J.) ("[T]he statute in <em>Zadvydas</em> ... served the purpose of preventing aliens ... from fleeing prior to actual deportation").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent concedes that "[t]he scheme considered in <em>Zadvydas</em> did not provide review immediately ...&nbsp;. [C]ustody review hearings usually occurred within three months of a transfer to a postorder detention unit."  <em>Post</em>, at 17, n.&nbsp;11 (opinion of <em>Souter</em>, J.).  Yet, in discussing the present case, the dissent insists that "the due process requirement of an individualized finding of necessity applies to detention periods shorter than" respondent's.  <em>Post</em>, at 30, n. 24 (citing <em>Schall</em> v. <em>Martin,</em> 467 U.&nbsp;S. 253 (1984), in which "the detainee was entitled to a hearing" when threatened with "a maximum detention period of 17 days").  The dissent makes no attempt to reconcile its suggestion that aliens are entitled to an immediate hearing with the holding in <em>Zadvydas</em> permitting aliens to be detained for several months prior to such a hearing.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The very limited time of the detention at stake under 1226(c) is not missed by the dissent.  See <em>post</em>, at 30 (opinion of <em>Souter</em>, J.) ("Successful challenges often require several months"); <em>post, </em>at 30,<em> </em>(considering "[t]he potential for several months [worth] of confinement"); but see <em>post, </em>at 10 ("potentially lengthy detention").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Congress has directed the INS to identify and track deportable criminal aliens while they are still in the criminal justice system, and to complete removal proceedings against them as promptly as possible.  See Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996, Pub. L. 104-32, 432, 438(a), 110 Stat. 1273-1276; Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Pub. L. 104-208, 326, 329, 110 Stat. 3009-630 to 3009-631 (codified at 8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1228).  The INS therefore established the Institutional Hearing Program (IHP) (subsequently subsumed under the "Institutional Removal Program").  By 1997, the General Accounting Office found that nearly half of all deportable criminal aliens' cases were completed through the IHP prior to the aliens' release from prison.  See General Accounting Office, Report to the Chairman, Subcommittee on Immigration and Claims of the House Committee on the Judiciary, INS' Efforts to Remove Imprisoned Aliens Continue to Need to Improve 10, Fig. 1 (Oct. 1998).  The report urged, however, that the INS needed to improve its operations in order to complete removal proceedings against all deportable criminal aliens before their release.  <em>Id</em>., at 13.  Should this come to pass, of course, 1226(c) and the temporary detention it mandates would be rendered obsolete.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Prior to the enactment of 1226(c), when the vast majority of deportable criminal aliens were not detained during their deportation proceedings, many filed frivolous appeals in order to delay their deportation.  See S.&nbsp;Rep. 104-48, at 2 ("Delays can earn criminal aliens more than work permits and wages--if they delay long enough they may even obtain U.&nbsp;S. citizenship").  Cf. <em>Zadvydas</em>, 402 U.&nbsp;S. 183, 213 (1971) (internal quotation marks omitted); accord, <em>Chaffin</em> v. <em>Stynchcombe,</em> 412 U.&nbsp;S. 17, 30-31 (1973).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Respondent was held in custody for three months before filing his habeas petition.  His removal hearing was scheduled to occur two months later, but respondent requested and received a continuance to obtain documents relevant to his withholding application.  See Brief for Respondent 9, n.&nbsp;12.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNother1.1" href="#FRother1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Kim does not claim a hearing before any specific official.  The generality of his claim may reflect the fact, noted just below, that the INS released him on bond without any hearing whatsoever after the District Court entered its judgment in this case.  App. 11-13.  Accordingly, there is no occasion to enquire whether due process requires access to any particular arbiter, such as one unaffiliated with the INS.  I therefore use the neutral term "impartial" in describing the hearing Kim claims.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNother1.2" href="#FRother1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In 1996, Congress combined "deportation" and "exclusion" proceedings into a single "removal" proceeding.  Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Pub. L. 104-208, 304(a), 110 Stat. 3009-587, adding 8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1229a.  Because this case requires consideration of cases decided both before and after 1996, this opinion refers to "removal" generally but, where the context requires, distinguishes between "deportation" of aliens who have entered the United States and "exclusion" of aliens who seek entry.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNother1.3" href="#FRother1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court's effort to explain its reference to a nonexistent concession, <em>ante</em>, at 11-12, n. 6, seeks to gain an advantage from the fact that the Immigration and Nationality Act uses the word "deportable" in various ways, one being to describe classes of aliens who may be removed if the necessary facts are proven, <em>e. g.</em>, 1227(a), and another to describe aliens who have actually been adjudged as being in the United States unlawfully, <em>e. g.</em>, 1229b.  An alien is not adjudged "deportable" until an order enters "concluding that the alien is deportable or ordering deportation," and such an order is not final until affirmed by the Board of Immigration Appeals or until the time expires for seeking review.  1101(a)(47)(A)-(B).  To suggest, as the Court seems to do, that an alien has conceded removability simply because he does not dispute that he has been charged with facts that will render him removable if those facts are later proven is like saying that a civil defendant has conceded liability by failing to move to dismiss the complaint under Federal Rule of Civil Procedure 12(b)(6) or that a criminal defendant has conceded guilt by failing to dispute the validity of the indictment.  But even if the Court's reasoning were sound, it would not cover Kim's situation, for he has stated (and the Court acknowledges) his intent to contest the sufficiency of his criminal convictions as a basis for removal.  <em>Ante</em>, at 11-12, n.&nbsp;6.  This discussion, which the Court calls a "detour," <em>ante</em>, at 12, is necessary only because of the Court's insistence in stating that Kim conceded that he is "deportable."  <em>Ante</em>, at 2, 11, 20.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNother1.4" href="#FRother1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although an LPR may seek exemption or discharge from registration on the grounds of alienage, such an action permanently bars the LPR from seeking United&nbsp;States citizenship.  8 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1426(a).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNother1.5" href="#FRother1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also <em>Welch </em>v. <em>Ashcroft</em>, 293 F.&nbsp;3d 213, 215 (CA4 2002) (detainee obtained LPR status at age 10); <em>Hoang</em> v. <em>Comfort</em>, 282 F.&nbsp;3d 1247, 1252-1253 (CA10 2002) (ages 3 and 15), cert. pending, No. 01-1616.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNother1.6" href="#FRother1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>The Venus,</em> 8 Cranch 253 (1814), we held that property belonging to American citizens who were resident in England during the War of 1812 was to be treated as belonging to English proprietors for purposes of prize law.  We stated that, as permanent residents of England, the American citizens were "bound, by such residence, to the society of which they are members, subject to the laws of the state, and owing a qualified allegiance thereto; they are obliged to defend it, (with an exception in favor of such a subject, in relation to his native country) in return for the protection it affords them, and the privileges which the laws bestow upon them as subjects," <em>id.</em>, at 282.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNother1.7" href="#FRother1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"Although the holding [in <em>Kwong Hai Chew</em>] was one of regulatory interpretation, the rationale was one of constitutional law.  Any doubts that <em>Chew</em> recognized constitutional rights in the resident alien returning from a brief trip abroad were dispelled by <em>Rosenberg</em> v. <em>Fleuti</em>, [374 U.&nbsp;S. 449 (1963),] where we described <em>Chew</em> as holding 'that the returning resident alien is entitled as a matter of due process to a hearing on the charges underlying any attempt to exclude him.'  459 U.&nbsp;S. 21, 33 (1982).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNother1.8" href="#FRother1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This case provides no occasion to determine the constitutionality of mandatory detention of aliens other than LPRs.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNother1.9" href="#FRother1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The statement that "[i]n the exercise of its broad power over naturalization and immigration, Congress regularly makes rules that would be unacceptable if applied to citizens," <em>Mathews</em> v. <em>Diaz,</em> 426 U.&nbsp;S. 67, 79-80 (1976), cannot be read to leave limitations on the liberty of aliens unreviewable.  <em>Ante</em>, at 10-11.  <em>Diaz</em> involved a federal statute that limited eligibility for a federal medical insurance program to United&nbsp;States citizens and LPRs who had been continuously resident in the United States for five years.  426 U.&nbsp;S., at 69-70.  Reversing a lower court judgment that this statute violated equal protection, we said this: </p>
<p>

"In the exercise of its broad power over naturalization and immigration, Congress regularly makes rules that would be unacceptable if applied to citizens.  The exclusion of aliens and the reservation of the power to deport have no permissible counterpart in the Federal Government's power to regulate the conduct of its own citizenry.  The fact that an Act of Congress treats aliens differently from citizens does not in itself imply that such disparate treatment is 'invidious.'&nbsp;"  <em>Id.</em>, at 79-<br>80 (footnotes omitted).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Taken in full, the meaning of this paragraph is plain: through the exercise of the deportation and exclusion power, Congress exposes aliens to a treatment (expulsion) that cannot be imposed on citizens.  The cases cited in the footnotes to this paragraph accordingly all concern Congress's power to enact grounds of exclusion or deportation.  <em>Id.</em>, at 80, nn. 14-15 (citing <em>Kleindienst</em> v. <em>Mandel,</em> 408 U.&nbsp;S. 753 (1972); <em>Galvan</em> v. <em>Press,</em> 347 U.&nbsp;S. 522 (1954); and <em>Harisiades</em> v. <em>Shaughnessy,</em> 342 U.&nbsp;S. 580 (1952)); cf. <em>ante</em>, at 10-11 (quoting <em>Diaz</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 81, n.&nbsp;17 (quoting <em>Harisiades</em>)).  Nothing in <em>Diaz</em> addresses due process protection of liberty or purports to sanction any particular limitation on the liberty of LPRs under circumstances comparable to those here.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even on its terms, the <em>Diaz </em>statement is dictum.  We acknowledged immediately that "[t]he real question presented by <em>[Diaz]</em> is not whether discrimination between citizens and aliens is permissible; rather, it is whether the statutory discrimination <em>within </em>the class of aliens--allowing benefits to some aliens but not to others--is permissible."  426 U.&nbsp;S., at 80.  Our holding that Congress could consider length of residence and immigration status in allocating medical insurance in no way suggests the existence of a federal power to imprison a long-term resident alien when the Government concedes that there is no need to do so.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court does not explain why it believes the <em>Diaz </em>dictum to be relevant to this case, other than to repeat it and identify prior instances of its quotation.  <em>Ante</em>, at 10-11.  The Court resists calling the statement "&nbsp;'dictum,'&nbsp;" <em>ante</em>, at 10, but it does not deny that <em>Diaz</em> involved "discrimination <em>within</em> the class of aliens" rather than "discrimination between citizens and aliens," 426 U.&nbsp;S., at 80, thus making any suggestion about Congress's power to treat citizens and aliens differently unnecessary to the holding.  Nor does the Court deny that <em>Diaz</em> dealt with an equal protection challenge to the allocation of medical insurance and had nothing to say on the subject of the right of LPRs to protection of their liberty under the Due Process Clause.  See <em>supra</em>, at 4-9.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNother1.10" href="#FRother1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In support of its standard, the dissent relied on a report by the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees, which likewise countenanced detention only "in cases of necessity" and stated, under a heading entitled "Guideline 3: Exceptional Grounds for Detention":</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"There should be a presumption against detention.  Where there are monitoring mechanisms which can be employed as viable alternatives to detention, (such as reporting obligations or guarantor requirements ...), these should be applied <em>first</em> unless there is evidence to suggest that such an alternative will not be effective in the individual case.  Detention should therefore only take place after a full consideration of all possible alternatives, or when monitoring mechanisms have been demonstrated not to have achieved the lawful and legitimate purpose."  United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees, Guidelines on Applicable Criteria and Standards Relating to the Detention of Asylum Seekers (Feb. 1999) (hereinafter Detention Guidelines) (emphasis in original), cited in <em>Zadvydas</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 721 (opinion of <em>Kennedy</em>, J.).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The High Commissioner also referred to the "minimum procedural guarante[e]" for a detainee "either personally or through a representative, to challenge the necessity of the deprivation of liberty at the review hearing, and to rebut any findings made."  Detention Guidelines, Guideline 5: Procedural Safeguards.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNother1.11" href="#FRother1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>The scheme considered in <em>Zadvydas</em> did not provide review immediately after the removability determination; the dissent noted that custody review hearings usually occurred within three months of a transfer to a postorder detention unit, with further reviews annually or more frequently if the alien requested them.  533 U.&nbsp;S., at 722-723.  But the lag was fitted to the circumstances.  In the usual case, removal in fact would come promptly; it is only when it did not that interim custody raised a substantial issue.  The issue here, of course, is not timing but the right to individualized review at all.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNother1.12" href="#FRother1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The hearing recognized in <em>Matter of Joseph</em>, 22 I.&nbsp;&amp;&nbsp;N. Dec. 799 (BIA 1999), is no response to this deficiency.  As the Court notes, the "&nbsp;'<em>Joseph </em>hearing'&nbsp;" only permits an alien to show that he does not meet the statutory criteria for mandatory detention under 1226(c).  <em>Ante</em>, at 2-3, and n.&nbsp;3.  Kim argues that, even assuming that he fits under the statute, the statute's application to LPRs like him does not fit under the Due Process Clause.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Justice Kennedy</em> recognizes that the Due Process Clause requires "an individualized determination as to [an LPR's] risk of flight and dangerousness if the continued detention [becomes] unreasonable or unjustified."  <em>Ante</em>, at 2 (concurring opinion).  It is difficult to see how Kim's detention in this case is anything but unreasonable and unjustified, since the Government concedes that detention is not necessary to completion of his removal proceedings or to the community's protection.  Certainly the fact that "there is at least some merit to the [INS's] charge" that Kim should be held to be removable, <em>ante</em>, at 1, does not establish a compelling reason for detention.  The INS releases many noncriminal aliens on bond or on conditional parole under 1226(a)(2) pending removal proceedings, and the fact that Kim has been convicted of criminal offenses does not on its own justify his detention, see <em>supra</em>, at 12-14.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNother1.13" href="#FRother1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Welch </em>v. <em>Ashcroft</em>, 293 F.&nbsp;3d 213 (CA4 2002); <em>Hoang</em> v. <em>Comfort</em>, 282 F.&nbsp;3d 1247 (CA10 2002), cert. pending, No. 01-1616; <em>Patel</em> v. <em>Zemski</em>, 275 F.&nbsp;3d 299 (CA3 2001).  The Seventh Circuit's decision in <em>Parra</em> v. <em>Perryman</em>, 172 F.&nbsp;3d 954 (1999), preceded our decision in <em>Zadvydas</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNother1.14" href="#FRother1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although Kim is a convicted criminal, we are not concerned here with a State's interest in punishing those who violate its criminal laws.  Kim completed the criminal sentence imposed by the California courts on February 1, 1999, and California no longer has any interest in incarcerating him.    </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNother1.15" href="#FRother1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; The Court's citations to <em>Wong Wing</em> v. <em>United States,</em> 163 U.&nbsp;S. 228 (1896), are therefore inapposite.  <em>Ante</em>, at 12, 20.  In <em>Wong Wing</em>, we hypothesized that detention "necessary to give effect" to the removal of an alien "would be valid"; the use of the subjunctive mood makes plain that the issue was not before the Court.  163 U.&nbsp;S., at 235.<em>  Wong Wing</em> certainly did not hold that detention in aid of removal was exempt from the Due Process Clause.</p>
<p>

<em>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</em>Moreover, the <em>Wong Wing </em>dictum must be understood in light of the common contemporary practice in the federal courts of releasing aliens on bail pending deportation proceedings.  While the Court is correct that the first statutory provision permitting Executive officials to release aliens on bond was enacted in 1907, <em>ante</em>, at 12, n.&nbsp;7, the Court ignores the numerous judicial grants of bail prior to that year.  See,<em> e.</em>&nbsp;<em>g.</em>, <em>United States ex rel. Turner</em> v. <em>Williams,</em> 194 U.&nbsp;S. 279, 283 (1904) (stating that the lower court admitted the appellant to bail pending appeal to this Court); <em>Fong Yue Ting</em> v. <em>United States,</em> 149 U.&nbsp;S. 698, 704 (1893) (same); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Moy Yee Tai</em>, 109 F. 1 (CA2 1901); <em>In&nbsp;re Lum Poy</em>, 128 F. 974, 975 (CC Mont. 1904) (noting that "the practice in California, Idaho, and Oregon has been and is to admit Chinese persons to bail pending an investigation into the lawfulness of their residence within the United States, and before any order for deportation has been made"); <em>In&nbsp;re Ah Tai</em>, 125 F. 795, 796-797 (Mass. 1903) (identifying a practice in several federal districts admitting aliens to bail, both before an initial finding of deportability and during the appeal therefrom); <em>In&nbsp;re Chow Goo Pooi</em>, 25 F. 77, 78 (CC Cal. 1884).
