The breadth of this practice is evident from one court's statement that "[t]o hold bail altogether inadmissible . . . would invalidate hundreds of existing recognizances."  <em>Ah Tai</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 797.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As Judge Augustus Hand later noted, the only change in 1907 was that bail decisions were committed to the discretion of Executive officials, rather than judges: </p>
<blockquote><p>"Prior to the passage by Congress in 1907 of the act empowering the administrative official to fix bail, various courts made it a practice to grant bail to aliens during deportation hearings. . . .  In our opinion that act was intended to place the general determination of granting bail in the hands of the authorities charged with the enforcement of the deportation laws as persons ordinarily best qualified to perform such a function . . . ."  <em>United States ex rel.</em> <em>Potash</em> v. <em>District Director of Immigration and Naturalization</em>, 169 F.&nbsp;2d 747, 751 (CA2 1948) (citations omitted).</p>
<p>

Thus, while <em>Wong Wing </em>stated in passing that detention may be used where it was "part of the means necessary" to the removal of aliens, 163 U.&nbsp;S., at 235, that statement was written against the background of the general availability of judicial relief from detention pending deportation proceedings.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The judicial grants of bail prior to 1907 arose in federal habeas proceedings.  Contrary to <em>Justice O'Connor</em>'s objection to federal jurisdiction in this matter, there is indeed a "history of routine reliance on habeas jurisdiction to challenge the detention of aliens without bail pending the conclusion of removal proceedings."  <em>Ante</em>, at 4 (opinion concurring in part and concurring in judgment).</p>
</blockquote>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNother1.16" href="#FRother1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court tellingly does not even mention <em>Salerno</em>, <em>Foucha</em>, <em>Hendricks</em>, or <em>Jackson</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNother1.17" href="#FRother1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court is therefore mistaken in suggesting that I view the detention of the individual aliens in <em>Zadvydas</em> as serving a governmental purpose.  <em>Ante</em>, at 17, n.&nbsp;10.  The Court confuses the "statute in <em>Zadvydas</em>, viewed outside the context of any individual alien's detention," <em>supra</em>, at 23, with the "detention at issue in <em>Zadvydas</em>," <em>ante</em>, at 17, n.&nbsp;10, namely the detention of Zadvydas and Ma as individuals.  The due process analysis in <em>Zadvydas</em> concentrated on the latter, holding that the detention of Zadvydas and Ma would not serve a legitimate immigration purpose if there were no "significant likelihood of removal in the reasonably foreseeable future."  533 U.&nbsp;S., at 701.  Thus, the Court's suggestion in this case that "the statutory provision" authorizes "detention" that prevents deportable aliens from fleeing as a general matter, <em>ante</em>, at 17, is no sufficient basis for claiming <em>Zadvydas</em> as support for the Court's methodology or result.  Rather, the Court should consider whether the detention of Kim as an individual is necessary to a compelling Government interest, just as it did for the detention of Zadvydas and Ma as individuals.  As the Government concedes, Kim's individual detention serves no Government purpose at all.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNother1.18" href="#FRother1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Nor can the general risk of recidivism, <em>ante</em>, at 7, justify this measure.  The interest in preventing recidivism may be vindicated "by the ordinary criminal processes involving charge and conviction, the use of enhanced sentences for recidivists, and other permissible ways of dealing with patterns of criminal conduct."  <em>Foucha</em> v. <em>Louisiana,</em> 504 U.&nbsp;S. 71, 82 (1992).  The ability to detain aliens in removal proceedings who pose threats to the community also satisfies this interest.  Cf. <em>United States</em> v. <em>Salerno,</em> 481 U.&nbsp;S. 739 (1987).  The alternative to detention, of course, is not unrestricted liberty, but supervised release, which also addresses the risk of recidivism.  <em>Zadvydas</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 696.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNother1.19" href="#FRother1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Detention of such aliens is governed by the statute at issue in<em> Zadvydas</em>, 1231(a), not by 1226(c).&nbsp;</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNother1.20" href="#FRother1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A prior study by the same body noted that nonappearance rates by aliens in deportation proceedings before issuance of orders to deport (aliens, that is, like Kim) were approximately 23% for the first half of 1993 and 21% for all of 1992.  Department of Justice, Office of the Inspector General, Case Hearing Process in the Executive Office for Immigration Review, Rep. No. I-93-03, p. 5 (May 1994) (hereinafter Case Hearing Report).  Congress appears to have considered these relevant figures, Senate Report, at 2 ("Over 20 percent of nondetained criminal aliens fail to appear for deportation proceedings"), without referring to irrelevant post-order numbers.  The Government relied on the Post-Order Report in its brief and at oral argument.  Brief for Petitioners 7, 19-20, and n.&nbsp;7; Tr. of Oral Arg. 23.  The Government did not cite the Case Hearing Report.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNother1.21" href="#FRother1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court throws in minor criticisms of the Vera Institute Study that have no bearing on its relevance here.  The Institute's supervised release program included 127 criminal aliens who would be subject to mandatory detention under 1226(c) because of their criminal histories.  Vera Institute Study 33.  Since the INS seeks Kim's removal on the grounds of either crimes of moral turpitude or an aggravated felony, see <em>ante</em>, at 1-2, n.&nbsp;1, the fact that most of the Vera Institute Study's subjects were convicted of crimes of moral turpitude but not an aggravated felony, <em>ante</em>, at 9, n.&nbsp;5, is of no moment.  Nor were all of the aliens studied subject to intensive supervision, <em>ibid.</em>; most were subject to "regular supervision," which involved no mandatory reporting sessions beyond an initial orientation session with supervision staff and required only that the alien keep the staff apprised of a current mailing address, appear in court, and comply with the orders of the immigration judge.  Vera Institute Study 17-18.  That the Institute considered various screening criteria before authorizing supervised release, <em>ante</em>, at 9, n.&nbsp;5, does not undermine the value of the study, since any program adopted by the INS in lieu of mandatory detention could do the same.  Cf. <em>Zadvydas</em>, 533 U.&nbsp;S., at 696.  Finally, the fact that Kim sought and was granted release on bond rather than supervised release, <em>ante</em>, at 9, n.&nbsp;5, does not detract from the relevance of the Vera Institute Study.  Regardless of what methods the INS decides to employ to prevent flight, the study supports the conclusion that mandatory detention under 1226(c) is "not necessary" to prevent flight, Vera Institute Study 42, and therefore violates the Due Process Clause.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNother1.22" href="#FRother1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This case accordingly presents no issue of "&nbsp;'court ordered release,'&nbsp;" <em>ante</em>, at 20, n. 14 (quoting <em>Zadvydas</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 713 (<em>Kennedy</em>, J., dissenting)); in this case, for example, the INS reached its own determination to release Kim on bond.  This case concerns only the uncontroversial requirement that detention serve a compelling governmental interest and that detainees be afforded adequate procedures ensuring against erroneous confinement.  <em>E. g.</em>, <em>United States</em> v. <em>Salerno,</em> 481 U.&nbsp;S. 739, 751 (1987) ("[T]he procedures by which a judicial officer evaluates the likelihood of future dangerousness are specifically designed to further the accuracy of that determination"); see also <em>Zadvydas</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 721 (<em>Kennedy</em>, J., dissenting) (stating that due process requires "adequate procedures" permitting detained aliens to show that "they no longer present special risks or danger" warranting confinement).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNother1.23" href="#FRother1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Criminal aliens whose "removal proceedings are completed while [they are] still serving time for the underlying conviction," <em>ante</em>, at 19, are irrelevant to this case, since they are never detained pending removal proceedings under 1226(c).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNother1.24" href="#FRother1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court calls several months of unnecessary imprisonment a "very limited time," <em>ante</em>, at 19, n.&nbsp;12.  But the due process requirement of an individualized finding of necessity applies to detention periods shorter than Kim's.  <em>Schall</em> v. <em>Martin,</em> 467 U.&nbsp;S. 253 (1984), involved a maximum detention period of 17 days, <em>id.</em>, at 270, yet our due process analysis noted that the detainee was entitled to a hearing in which he could challenge the necessity of his confinement before an impartial decisionmaker required to state the facts and reasons underlying any decision to detain, <em>id.</em>, at 276-277.  The 90-day removal period in 1231(a)(1) not only has a fixed endpoint, but also applies only after the alien has been adjudged removable, 1231(a)(1)(B).  The discussion <br>of that provision in <em>Zadvydas</em> cannot be read to indicate any standard of permissible treatment of an LPR who has not yet been found <br>removable.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNother1.25" href="#FRother1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Similarly, the question presented in<em> Butterfield</em> v. <em>Zydok</em>, argued and decided together with <em>Carlson</em>, was "[w]hether, in exercising his discretion to grant or withhold bail pending final determination of the deportability of an alien, the Attorney General is justified in denying bail on the ground that the alien is an active participant in Communist Party affairs, or whether he is bound also to consider other circumstances, particularly the likelihood that the alien will report as ordered."  Pet. for Cert. in <em>Butterfield</em> v. <em>Zydok</em>, O. T. 1951, No. 136, p.&nbsp;2.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNother1.26" href="#FRother1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;While a prior conviction may sometimes evidence a risk of future danger, it is not conclusive in all cases, and Kim is a good example, given that the Government found that he "would not be considered a threat."  App. 13.  Indeed, the Court acknowledges that convictions are only "relevant to" dangerousness, <em>ante</em>, at 15, n.&nbsp;9; it does not state that they compel a finding of danger in all cases.  As even the <em>Zadvydas</em> dissent recognized, due process requires that detained criminal aliens be given an opportunity to rebut the necessity of detention by showing "that through rehabilitation, new appreciation of their responsibilities, or under other standards, they no longer present special risks or danger if put at large."  533 U.&nbsp;S., at 721 (opinion of <em>Kennedy</em>, J.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNother1.27" href="#FRother1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In the footnote immediately following its citation of dissenting opinions, the Court cites a passage from the <em>Carlson</em> majority opinion confirming that the <em>Carlson </em>petitioners' detention rested on the "allegation, supported by affidavits, that the [INS's] dossier of each petitioner contained evidence" of Communist Party membership and activities "to the prejudice of the public interest."  342 U.&nbsp;S., at 530 (quoted <em>ante</em>, at 14, n.&nbsp;9).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNother1.28" href="#FRother1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Justice Black's dissenting statement that one of the aliens was "&nbsp;'not likely to engage in any subversive activities,'&nbsp;" 342 U.&nbsp;S., at 549, does not amount to a "specific finding of non-dangerousness," <em>ante</em>, at 14.  On the contrary, the Court expressly stated that the Government could prove dangerousness based on "personal activity" in the Communist Party; it simply was not required to go so far as to show "specific acts of sabotage or incitement to subversive action."  <em>Carlson</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 541.  Thus while there was no finding of "subversive action," there certainly was a finding of "danger," albeit one that Justice Black found unconvincing.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Likewise, Justice Frankfurter's statement in dissent that the Solicitor General of the United States had "advised" that "it has been the Government's policy ... to terminate bail" for aliens awaiting deportation who were "present active Communists," 342 U.&nbsp;S., at 568, is difficult to reconcile with the contrary statements in both the majority opinion and the United States' brief in <em>Carlson</em>, see <em>supra</em>, at 31-33.  Whatever its basis, Justice Frankfurter's reference to a "policy" of bail denials does not bear the weight that the Court places upon it today. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNother1.29" href="#FRother1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Nor is it to the point for the Court to quote <em>Flores</em> as rejecting the aliens' challenge to a "&nbsp;'&nbsp;"blanket" presumption of the unsuitability of custodians other than parents, close relatives, and guardians.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ante</em>, at 15 (quoting 507 U.&nbsp;S., at 313).  <em>Flores</em> expressly stated that the regulation did not implicate the core liberty interest in avoiding physical confinement.  <em>Id.</em>, at 302 ("The 'freedom from physical restraint' .&nbsp;. . is not at issue in this case").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNother1.30" href="#FRother1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, the passages the Court quotes from <em>Flores</em> did not concern the regulation's constitutionality at all, but rather its validity as an implementation of the authorizing statute.  <em>Id.</em>, at 313 ("Respondents also contend that the INS regulation violates the statute because it relies upon a 'blanket' presumption").  <em>Flores </em>clearly separated its analysis of the regulation under the Due Process Clause from its analysis of the regulation under the statute.  See <em>id.</em>, at 300; see also <em>id.</em>, at 318-319 (<em>O'Connor, J., </em>concurring) (pointing out the substantive due process analysis at <em>id.</em>, at 301-306, and the procedural due process analysis at <em>id.</em>, at 306-309).</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

<div class="caseInformation section">

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> DEMORE v. KIM, 538 U.S. 510 (2003)</h2>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Citation:</b> 538 U.S. 510</p>

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

  }

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);
    linksWithSearchLocation.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = e.currentTarget.dataset.searchLocation || e.currentTarget.dataset.closest("[data-search-location]").dataset.searchLocation;
        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
